CEREMONIAL RESOLUTIONS
RESOLUTION
NO. 22-1
CONGRATULATING T. KOMODA STORE &
BAKERY OF MAKAWAO, MAUI, HAWAN ON ITS
IO5TH ANNIVERSARY
CHAIR LEE: Councilmember Mike Molina.
COUNCILMEMBER MOLINA: Mahalo and good morning, Madam Chair.
I MOVE TO ADOPT THE RESOLUTION "CONGRATULATING
T. KOMODA STORE & BAKERY OF MAKAWAO, MAUI,
HAWAN ON ITS 105'^^ ANNIVERSARY".
COUNCILMEMBER SUGIMURA:
SECOND.
COUNCILMEMBER KAMA: Second.
CHAIR LEE: Moved by Member Molina, seconded by Member Sugimura, to adopt the
Resolution 22-1.
Discussion, Member Molina?
COUNCILMEMBER MOLINA: Thank you very much. Madam Chair. May we have the Clerk
read the resolution?
CHAIR LEE: Madam Clerk.
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
January 7, 2022
Page 9
(The resolution was read in its entirety.)
CHAIR LEE: Member Molina.
COUNCILMEMBER MOLINA: Thank you very much, Madam Chair. First of ail, I'd like to
start off by thanking my staff and also Committee staff for preparing this resolution, and
to the Komoda's for graciously accepting this resolution or letting us do this resolution
for them.
This family and organization is a shining example of a very successful mom-and-pop
small business. It survived many historical events and economic downturns. Komoda
Store has brought happiness to the taste buds of generations of Upcountry, Maui
County, and State residents, as well as tourists from around the world. Komoda Store
& Bakery is truly a destination. No trip to Maui is complete without a visit to Komoda
Store.
Now, while we all enjoy their legendary products, which include stick donuts, butter
roils, and cream puffs, let's not forget the hard work, care and love that's put into these
products by the Komoda family and associates over the years.
As the Clerk mentioned, Komoda Store had very humble beginnings, starting off first
as a restaurant feeding plantation workers and cowboys back in the early 1900's and
then evolving into a bakery in 1947. And it takes  alot to survive as a bakery these
days, especially when competing against corporate businesses like Love's Bakery and
Krispy Kreme. But like many, we appreciate Komoda Store staying true to their roots,
as a small, humble, local family style store that's maintains the charm of our small
historical towns.
So, in conclusion, congratulations to the Komoda's and Shibuya's, and ail the rest of
their ohanas for this 105‘^ Anniversary celebration. And it is my hope that Komoda
Store can someday celebrate another 105 years of success. Mahalo, Madam Chair.
CHAIR LEE: Thank you. Any more discussion?
Member Suglmura.
COUNCILMEMBER SUGIMURA: Yeah, thank you. 1 too really appreciate Komoda Store.
And they're a big part of the Makawao community. And my experience with them is, i
see how much they give back, right? Here they're  astore that they sell out really fast,
they, the whole family gets up super early. Mike told me his stories about that. And
yet, they give to the, when the, this is pre-COVID when there was a veteran's ceremony
at the Makawao Veterans Cemetery. They would provide pastries for everybody who
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
January 7, 2022
Page 10
showed up to go to the memorial service. So, we have boxes and boxes of these
donations from this family. You know, they're always giving back to the community,
helping Makawao Elementary down the street. But I have a, oh, and Third Friday,
when the town would open up, they would open up their parking lot and, you know,
they were present, they were ever present.
But I have a personal story about them, is that I shop there periodically and buy things
when I go to meetings. And one day, I walked in and their daughter said, "we owe you
money". And I said, "you do?". And I said, "how come?". And I guess I must have
walked away after I bought something, and I didn't take the change. And do you know,
I don't even know when it was, but they gave me back change. Amazing, right?
Integrity, honesty, they’re a small store, and I just couldn't believe they did that. But
that is really the flavor of the town. And I appreciate the Komoda family for what they
do, so I fully support this resolution.
Mr. Molina, thank you very much for doing this. Well-deserved family to be recognized
for what they've given to us for many, many years. So, thank you.
CHAIR LEE: Member King.
COUNCILMEMBER KING; Thank you. Chair. And thank you, Member Molina, for bringing
this forward, 105 years, oh my goodness. First of all, I want to point out the irony of
half of us making New Year's resolutions to lose weight and our first resolution of the
year is to honor Maui's favorite donut shop. That's all ironic.
But having said that, i, I want to recognize that, that the Komoda's Bakery is part of two
things we're trying to do here. One is diversify the economy, because this is a type of
industry and local business that will survive with or without tourists, I believe. The
other, I don't know if I dropped of there. Can you still hear me?
CHAIR LEE: Yes.
COUNCILMEMBER KING: The other, the other part of it is it's part of the circular economy.
And so, what we want to do going forward is promote more local business, promote
people who give back to the community and who are part of our ongoing circular
economy where the, where the revenues stays in the community, and the people who
are benefitting from this business, they're all part of the community. So, thank you for
150 years of that, to Komoda Store. And even though i don't get up there very often, I
do enjoy those cream puffs whenever somebody brings them into a meeting. So, just
appreciate you personally, but also, on the larger scale as a really important part of our
ongoing economy. Thank you. Chair.

January 7, 2022
Page 11
CHAIR LEE: Thank you.
Members? Member Kama.
COUNCILMEMBER KAMA: Oh gosh, I just want to share  astory. It was just so funny thinking
about it. You know, when my dad was still alive, he was the president of the VFW.
And we would always go to Makawao Cemetery on Veterans Day. And so, because
he went and my brothers and sisters were vets, and we were military family, we'd all
go and take all my kids. And it got to the point where my son, he would say, "Ma, going
get Komoda Bakery up there?". I said, "Son, we're not going there to eat pastries.
We're going to honor the vets who have died for your freedom". He says, "yeah, I
know, Ma, but they going get?" I thought, I don't know, but I'm not going for that, son.
So, ever since then, now my son has moved away, he doesn't live here anymore, but
when he does come home, that's the first place he goes, is Komoda's. And you know,
I think that what small stores like this do for us, is that they create these memories in
our hearts, and they create these wonderful feelings of who we are as a community
and how that community extends out and beyond just Makawao. So, I'm just so grateful
for Member Molina for bringing this forward, and for the Komoda Bakery. And I do
hope you stay here for the next 200 years. So, thank you, Chair. And thank you,
Member Molina.
CHAIR LEE: Thank you. Pro Tern Kama. Anybody else want to say a few words about-

Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
January 7, 2022
Page 24
CSLFRF and NEUs. We are currently awaiting the second half of those monies to be
dispersed, forgive me for not knowing the limitations of how the money can be spent
as we move further. I would like the Council to consider investing in mental health
services, creative housing solutions, and support services for our community.
Creative solutions are not pipe dreams and should not be downgraded as sparkles and
fantasies. Previous solutions are about working with what you have to provide
solutions in desperate times. And if you don't feel like we are living in desperate times
now you soon will. Boxing ourselves in by implementing big city ideas without the
flexibility of recognizing the limitations of living in a rural community on finite land will
not address our housing crisis adequately. Ignoring the impact poor mental health has
on a person's capacity to function at the levels expected to obtain and retain housing
is heartless and detrimental to our community as  awhole.
I ask that some of these monies be used to acquire land for transitional housing and
encampments, increase trauma and form behavioral health programs, tackle the digital
divide for our no income and low-income residents ,and increase access to health care,
especially as it relates to COVID, mental and behavioral health. The reality is, is we're
going to see the impact of COVID that extends well beyond the physical symptoms,
and we need to be prepared. People on the lowest rungs of the income brackets are
already suffering and dying because of the lack of access to basic resources. And
know that many of our residents are standing at the precipice of stability. Please
consider using the Federal monies to address these concerns.

becoming a lawyer because I love a good argument. So you can imagine my
disappointment when I read the Mayor's reasonings for vetoing the moratorium bill. He
gave us three reasons, unintended consequences, conversion of apartments to short
term rentals, and lack of transparency and public input.
As a resident of Maui County, I would like to address his three concerns:
1) Unintended consequences of residents renting out personal vehicles due to the
shortfall of rental vehicles. The rental company shipped out their cars and did not ship
them back. Once the numbers came up, the residents capitalized on that need and
the monies went straight into the pockets of our local residents, not to the big rental
companies, and I find that kind of interesting.
2) The moratorium may encourage people to convert their residential units to short
term rental units, which is a good point. I wonder if there's a body of people charged
with protecting residents from predatory behaviors stoked by the tourism industry that
could create laws to make sure that we're safe. Like maybe their title could literally be

MR. CHRISTOPHER DELAUNAY, PACIFIC RESOURCE PARTNERSHIP (testifying on
County Communication No. 22-9):
Aloha, Councilmembers. Chris Delaunay with Pacific Resource Partnership. I'm a
registered lobbyist and I'm here to testify in opposition to Bill 148, declaring a
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
January 7, 2022
Page 30
moratorium on new transient accommodations on Maui. I respectfully request that this
Council uphold the Mayor's veto of this bill.
A moratorium is not going to stop or limit the amount of people from traveling to Maui.
This moratorium will not prevent airplanes from bringing people to Maui. It will not
benefit Maui's economy, and it will not alleviate Maui's housing crisis. Instead, if
enacted into law, Bill 148 will likely push visitors to seek accommodations outside of
lands or areas designated for resort use to Maui's residential neighborhoods. Visitors
will stay at legal or illegal short-term rentals in Maui's neighborhoods which will only
erode the quality of life for Maui residents. If homeowners rent their homes to visitors,
this will equate to fewer housing units for Maui residents which will further intensify
Maui's housing crisis.
Lastly limiting visitor accommodations will not stimulate Maui's economy. Instead, it
only limits the growth of other industries like construction, retail, restaurants, and many
small businesses that provide services and goods to hotels within resort districts. Let's
keep Maui, Maui's residential neighborhoods for Maui residents, and allow Maui hotels
to make improvements to provide accommodations to visitors within designated hotel
districts. Thank you.

MR. JONATHAN LIKEKE SCHEUER (testifying on Resolution No. 22-4):
Good morning, aloha. Chair Lee. Vice-Chair Keani Rawlins and Members of the
Council. My name is Jonathan Likeke Scheuer. I'm here today on behalf of William
Aila and the Department of Hawaiian Home Lands, testifying on Resolution 22-4,
regarding the County trying to obtain the East Maui Irrigation license or lease. So we've
submitted written testimony to you. I appreciate the long day that you have ahead of
you, and will try and summarize the testimony quickly.
DHHL and its beneficiaries, when the State leases water, has three distinct major
interests in the water. The first is that it's a requirement of State law that prior to the
State leasing any water, a reservation of an adequate amount for future uses for
DHHL's needs be set aside prior to the issuance of the lease. The second distinct
interest due to provisions in the Hawaiian Homes Commission Act, the State
Constitution and Statute is that 30 percent of all the revenue from any lease be provided
to the Department into the Native Hawaiian Rehabilitation Fund. And finally, of course,
our beneficiaries also may exercise traditional and customary practices related to any
of the waters that are diverted.
In the case of the East Maui system, we have three aina that are affected by that
system. We have lands at Keanae and Wailuanui, which historically were deprived of
water by that system, but thanks to the Water Commission's action, now have full flow
and water flowing to those lands. We have lands at Keokea and Waiohuli, over
6,000 acres which are in need of water, and we have lands in Pulehunui in Central
Maui.
Working with the Water Commission, the Department of Land and Natural Resources,
and Mahi Pono, EMI, Alexander and Baldwin, and our beneficiaries, we developed a
request for approximately 11-million-gallon day reservation from these systems to our
lands at Keokea, Waiohuli, and Pulehunui, and we've submitted that for approval to the
Water Commission. So we are waiting for action from the Water Commission on that.
And we recognize and are pleased that the resolution acknowledges the need to
provide water to DHHL. We're supportive of the intent of the resolution and would note
that A&B and EMI and Mahi Pono have not indicated to us any displeasure or, or
opposition to our resolution, but we do recognize that the very existence of Maui County
and the very existence of the State of Hawaii is dependent on a Compact with the
United States, to faithfully uphold the Hawaiian Homes Commission Act and implement
it. And that obligation is very clear to any State agency and subdivision of the State.
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
January 7, 2022
Page 37
So we recognize the benefits to our beneficiaries that would accrue if the County were
to take over this lease. I'd be more than happy to answer any questions. Thank you
very much for listening to our testimony and reading our written. Mahalo.

limited funds and private sector developers often can swoop in and essentially get
water before we're able to get it to our beneficiaries. And so there's not a time limit on
how long it might take. But obviously we're very interested in doing so.

MR. BILL MEHEULA, (testifying on County Communication No. 22-9):
Aloha, Councilmembers. My name is Bill Meheula, I'm an attorney testifying on behalf

written testimony. I'm testifying on CC 22-9 in opposition to Bill 148 and in favor of the
Mayor's veto.
My only point is that Bill 148 violates due process guaranteed by the 14th Amendment,
as not rationally related to a legitimate State interest because the, the bill is legally
defective in three ways and only one defect is necessary to invalidate any law. The
three ways are the need for this moratorium is not factually justified. The need has not
been adequately investigated. And less extreme solutions have not been investigated.
The support for those legal defects are: 1) reliance on the Maui Island Plan, 4.2.3 as
a mandate for a visitor population should, that should not exceed 33 percent is not
justified. The reason why it's not justified is that section of the Maui Island Plan is
aspirational. It's ... it says roughly 33 percent and it's not a rule, you can't enforce it.
The other reason is that the Maui Island Plan was passed in 2012, and since then
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
January 7, 2022
Page 48
short-term rentals have exploded and hotel room production has gone down, it's
actually declined.
The second reason is that the bill itself actually admits that the bill is not, that the bill is
not necessary and less extreme solutions have not been adequately investigated
because right there in Section 19.98.10 it says that the moratorium may terminate upon
the TIGs recommendations on limits per transient accommodation type. That’s an
admission right in the bill that the adequate investigation has not been done.
The Council should at least wait until that TIG report comes out in three months before
moving on this type of broad moratorium. Thank you.

MS. CHAN HODGES: Good morning, my name is Shay Chan Hodges, and I am testifying in
support of Resos 22-4 and 5, both of which relate to our ability to protect our public
trust resource, water. Last month I testified before the Board of Water Supply about
Pretium Partners, a New York based investment firm whose business plan and
investors are revealed in the Pandora Papers with  atrove of offshore financial records
obtained by the International Consortium of Investigative Journalists.
The Washington Post had reported on how Pretium Partners . . . more than a billion
dollars for the Progress Residential real estate venture which reaped big profits from
stressed American renters amid the housing affordability crisis by buying up housing
and aggressively evicting tenants. Progress makes money by reducing costs on
maintenance and overhead and raising rents and charging fees. Congress is
investigating whether Progress violated a national moratorium on evictions during the
pandemic.
When I read the article, I recognized the names Pretium Partners and Progress
Residential from an article in the Toronto Star from last February. The title was why a
Canadian Crown corporation is banking on the downfall of the American Dream.
According to the article, as of last January, Pretium is in business to the tune of almost
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
January 7, 2022
Page 53
$900 million with a Canadian Crown corporation called the Public Sector Pension
Investment Board or PSP. As everyone knows by now, the Canadian Pension Fund
PSP is the sole owner of Mahi Pono, which is seeking full control of the EMI water
system and a 30-year lease to those public trust waters.
According to the Toronto Star, PSP's mission is to maximize returns without taking
undo risks, and they’re good at it. Canadian public pension funds generally are
extremely effective and are the envy of the world. The Pretium investment is just one
example of PSP values. PSP is also implicated in deaths, negligence, and tax
avoidance at the Revera elder care homes where they are the sole investor and
operator, and which operate in Canada where their own beneficiaries are cared for.
Clearly PSP's number one priority is making a sizable return on the investment.
Last month the Board of Water Supply voted unanimously to encourage the County
Council and the Mayor to pursue the four water leases in East Maui through an
intergovernmental agreement with DLNR, as recommended in the 2019 temporary
investigative report, which was also approved unanimously. In 2019, then State
Senator, now Congressman Kai Kahele wrote a letter to the Mayor recommending that
the County apply for the leases. I support these recommendations and encourage the
County Council to reach out to Ian Hirokawa, DLNR immediately because I strongly
believe that it is our kuliana to ensure that our land and water are protected, as we've
watched the contamination of the aquifer and suffering of Oahu families because of
Red Hill, it's obvious how necessary it is to proactively protect our water supply.
We elect our Mayor and Councilmembers for two and four year terms and as a result
they are accountable to us. We have no such recourse with a foreign entity. If the
Canadian Pension Fund controls our water for three decades, we abdicate our ability
to protect the public trust for ourselves, for our children, and their children. And after
that it may be too late. So please pass these resolutions today. Mahalo.

DEPUTY COUNTY CLERK JAMES KRUEGER: Chair the next person signed up to testify is
Kirsten Oleson. To be followed by Rob Weltman.
MS. KIRSTEN OLESON. (testifying on Resolution No. 22-4):
Aloha, I hope you can hear me. I am Kirsten Oleson, 1 am an associate professor of
ecological economics at the University of Hawaii at Manoa, with affiliate status at the
University of Hawaii Economic Research Organization and the Water Resources
Research Center. For over two decades my research has focused on incorporating
nature's contribution to human wellbeing into decisions.
I am pleased to provide testimony today in support of Resolution 22-4, related to Maui's
water. The resolution before you today offers a choice between managing Maui's water
either by providing it to the highest bidder in an auction or through a public entity
capable of recognizing the many values of water that are provided to the people of
Maui. Evaluating the worth of water is a notoriously difficult problem across the globe.
In 2021, the United Nations World Water Development Report noted that water's full
suite of values is rarely incorporated into decision-making. This failure to fully value
water is a root cause of its mismanagement. Our goal here should be a system that
can provide both sustainable and equitable water management. Valuing water as only
a financial commodity will not achieve that goal and is not the path forward to a thriving
Maui.
When I talk about the value of water. I'm not referring to the price of water charged and
when an economic transaction occurs. This price is often set at a level intended to
cover the costs of delivering clean water. But water is far more valuable than this.
Groundwater feed springs and serves as clean reservoirs of drinking water. Flowing
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
January 7, 2022
Page 61
surface water sustain loi and limu, and crashing waterfalls are sought after recreational
destinations.
The problem is that none of these values have obvious prices, so it's easy to ignore
them in decisions that are driven by financial returns. Unfortunately, we often
inadvertently trade off these highly valuable intangible benefits of water for more
tangible economic returns. To make things more difficult, these more tangible benefits
accrue to diverse stakeholders, while the tangible ones are often concentrated in the
interests of a few oversized players. This raises equity concerns.
We see these tradeoffs when land gets developed, reducing the watersheds capacity
to filter and store water when groundwater is extracted at rates greater than natural
recharge causing land subsidence and loss of traditional freshwater sources. And
when streams are diverted, leaving insufficient flow for instream and downstream uses.
So what is to be done? Water management decisions should reflect the full value of
water to society. Economic return should be one criterion, of course, but not to the
exclusion of the other benefits to society. As the UN report points out, those who control
how water is valued, control how it is used.
I support Resolution 22-4, because the County of Maui is charged with protecting the
public trust and therefore is uniquely able to balance waters many values. Mahalo.

MS. ANMARIE MABBUTT, (testifying on Countv Communication Nos. 22-6 and 22-7):
Good afternoon, Councilmembers. I'm speaking today on County Communication 22-6
and 22-7. I'm speaking as a resident, an open government advocate, and as someone
who loves Maui and is concerned for the health and safety of its residents.
CC 22-7 is a letter dated 12/10/21 from Finance Director Teruya informing the Council
of the County's receipt of its first installment of ARPA funds, $16,259,395. These funds
were received on May 19, 2021. Why did it take more than six months to inform the
Council of the receipt of these funds? The Fiscal Year 2022 Budget ordinance requires
the Administration to provide the Council at least once a month a report on the status
of efforts to obtain and expend Federal COVID relief funds. Do you know how the
County is spending the ARPA funds?
I  cannot find one instance in which the Council has included proposed ARPA
expenditures as a separate agenda item for a Council meeting. The neighbor islands
and Honolulu County have been openly and publicly discussing ARPA funding and
expenditures at properly noticed public Council meetings for more than six months.
Maui residents deserve to know how the County's ARPA funds are being spent and to
be given a meaningful opportunity to participate in that discussion.
Per the Fiscal Year 2022 First Quarterly Report, the County has spent $2,850,000 and
and encumbered 150,000 in ARPA funds, all for economic support. Specifically, a
$3 million grant to the Maui County Federal Credit Union for an economic support
program. MCFCU has already received $12,075,000 in County CARES Act funds,
which is 18.13 percent of the County's total CARES Act allocation, and almost 2 million
more than the total amount that the County spends on public health.
Mayor Victorino had promised to spend approximately $21 million in CARES Act funds
on public health, but according to the most recent CARES Act monthly report, as of
December 8, 21, the County has spent only 10,633,000 on public health. If you truly
care about the residents of Maui County, please do not allow what happened with the
CARES Act funds to happen again.
Based on a review of the County's monthly contracts/grants reports as of
November 30, 21, the County does not appear to have spent a single cent of ARPA
funds on public health. I do not see a single contract or grant paid for with ARPA funds.
What is happening with this money?
The past two weeks, every day hundreds of Maui residents have been struggling to
find testing. Many residents are currently waiting up to a week or more for a testing
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
January 7, 2022
Page 80
appointment and I have read far too many posts from parents who have sat waiting in
line for hours, with sick children to try to get  aCOVID test. This is not acceptable,
especially when it appears you've gone to efforts to achieve essential work status for
the Council, essential worker status for the Councilmembers so that you are able to
receive prioritized access to free quality testing.
During the August 25, 2021 private Council Chair meeting, Chair Lee said "we need to
make sure that our employees have access to testing--well, and preferably the quality
testing--as soon as possible. You know, we can't afford to be standing in line and so
forth." There was no mention of expanded quality testing for residents. We are in
crisis, the mistakes with the CARES Act funding cannot happen again. The Council
needs to prioritize public health over economic gain and tourism profits. The Council
should be actively publicly advocating to use ARPA funds to provide more free testing
to the public and free high-quality masks to residents, including the keiki, teachers, and
staff at the County schools. The Council should also advocate for using our ARPA
funds to pay for isolation and quarantine options, including hotel rooms, palate homes
and tents for any resident who needs it.
Moving onto my next item, CC 22-6, is a letter dated December 10, 2021, from Finance
Director Teruya, informing the Council of the receipt of an additional $8,761,000 in
ARPA funds on August 12, 2021. Again, why did it take Director Teruya more than
four months to inform the Council of the County's receipt of an additional $8,700,000
in ARPA funds. This delay clearly appears to violate Bill 46 (2021), the Fiscal Year
2022 Budget ordinance which requires the Administration to provide a report at least
once a month to the Council regarding the status of efforts to obtain and expend
Federal COVID relief funds. Based on the letters from Director Teruya, the County has
now received over $25 million in ARPA funds and plans to receive an additional
8,761,000 for a total of nearly $34 million.
Flow does the County plan to spend this money? Flave you asked the Mayor if he has
created a private economic taskforce, similar to his private CARES Act economic
taskforce to discuss the allocation of the ARPA funds? Mayor's economic taskforce for
the CARES Act met in secret for nearly four months before Mayor Victorino finally
revealed the identity of the taskforce members in July 2020. Five of the 21 members
of the Mayor's CARES Act economic taskforce represented organizations that received
over $21 million of the County's total CARES Act allocation. That is 31.58 percent.
Residents are concerned that as with the CARES Act funds, the decisions about how
the County's ARPA funds are being spent is happening behind closed doors. Maui
County residents deserve and need better.
To quote from the September 22, 2001 [sic] Council Chair meeting, Councilmember
Rawlins-Fernandez said "moving forward with the ARPA funds, I think, as Member

Aloha, Happy New Year, and shuprobhat. Chair Lee, Vice-Chair Rawlins-Fernandez
and Members of the Maui County Council. My name is Dave Jorgensen, I'm an
attorney in Wailuku. I'm a registered lobbyist and I'm testifying on behalf of the
American Resort Development Association of Hawaii, or ARDA Hawaii in strong
support of Mayor Victorino's veto of Bill 148.
The stated purpose of the bill is to establish a moratorium to comply with visitor to
resident ratio, as specified in Chapter 2.80.B of the Maui Island Plan by pausing the
increase of transient accommodations. However, there's no data to suggest that
stopping the development of transient accommodations will decrease the number of
visitors to Maui, and we believe it will not. Visitors will still come to Maui and seek other
accommodations, even if the supply of lodging units is limited. Visitors will seek lodging
in residential neighborhoods and long-term rental apartments and lead to an increase
in illegal short-term rentals. If the goal is to decrease the visitor count, more effective
regulation of legal and especially illegal short-term rental vacation units should be
pursued.
On the other hand, the moratorium will have a substantial economic impact on Maui's
economy, risking the substantial amounts of investments, tax revenues, and job
creation, for Maui's economy created by the tourist industry. For example, the
timeshare industry is currently a major contributor to Maui's economy by providing more
than $73 million per year in State and local taxes and supplying much needed jobs.
We should be mindful that the timeshare industry can be, can be a part of the solution.
The proposed moratorium seeks to maintain the status quo while we wait for the TIG
to study and present recommendations to the Council on how to address the tourist.

January 7, 2022
Page 92
tourism industry's impact on the environment and resident's quality of life. However,
given the potential negative economic impacts, there's no reason why a moratorium
must be imposed now rather than waiting until the TIG completes its work.

moratorium and Mr. Meheuta from Oahu summarized those earlier in his testimony.
Those issues still need to be addressed.

When we started this project nearly four years ago, we were looking to protect
150 acres of precious coastal lands in Hana, Maui worth over $10 million. We knew
as a unknown nonprofit no one was going to give us $10 million. But we thought if we
did, we thought, but we were determined to protect this aina and thought if we broke
this landmass into more manageable parcels with values closer to the funding sources
available to us, perhaps we might just over time be able to acquire these lands and
place them in conservation for our community. And that's exactly what we did.
We knew our two funding sources could support around $4 million or less, and so
essentially, we broke the 150 acres into three purchasable groups each valued at
$4 million. I've submitted our spreadsheet and a map showing the breakdown of these
lands into these parcel groups. To date we've acquired two of the three parcel groups
and we are very close to raising funds for the final parcel.
We're excited to visit, I was excited when I visited the Maka'alae lands shortly after Ke
Ao Hali'i completed this purchase. I sat on the aina, my legs stretched out in front of
me and my hands spread left and right, palms on the ground ... looking out across the
great Pacific where the ocean swell travel thousands of miles to reach the rugged rocky
windward coast and look and fix your delight in watching the dancing crashing waves.
I then glanced over my shoulder to the serene lush green pasture behind me in
Haleakala. As i sat there, I was overwhelmed by the realization that we have not just
won another battle to keep these lands from being developed, but we've actually
secured the lands from this fate forever, in perpetuity for potentially hundreds of years
these lands will be more or less . . . open space land with no development.
I don't think this has really sunk into our community, what's happened here? I don't
even think our board has quite understood it. but it's a really massive thing that we've
been able to do, and we could not have done it without the support of our partners and

so mahalo nui loa for your efforts in supporting this, we're so grateful to you.

consequences listening to this very body like the past 20 years. It's the unintended
consequences of a moratorium bill that is my concern. There's the business standpoint
that it would hurt a lot of locally owned businesses whose livelihood depends on the
visitor industry. Local farmers whose main clientele is hotels. Locally owned shops,
restaurants, stores and lunch wagons, the charter boats, the vans, bus ... all impacted
by COVID, and most are still trying to recover that we know of.
The problem is the moratorium bill, whose intent is to address trying to limit the number
of visitors to the island by putting a limit on new hotel rooms from being built doesn't
really address the issue of overtourism. The reason is the bill doesn't limit, nor can it
put a limit on the number of flights to the islands. The bill doesn't limit the number of
passengers on these flights to the islands. What the bill does unintendedly is put a
limit on where they can stay. And this is areas already designated for hotel and resorts.
That's where we want them to stay. Hotel districts provide limits by not allowing hotels
outside of the district. This is something that the Council, Planning Commission,
people of Maui, GPAC, have agreed upon through community plans and legislation.
So if you put a cap on the number of rooms in an area already designated hotel and
resort, then the question is where are they going to stay? They're going to go on
Trivago, Expedia, KAYAK, Travelocity, and look for a bed and breakfast and a short
term vacation rental. They're going to rent a house, cottage, apartment, condo. The
unintended consequence is where is this house, cottage, apartment or condo located?
1 can tell you where it's not. It's not in a resort or hotel zoned area. It's in our
neighborhoods. That's another unintended consequence of this moratorium bill. Every
house, cottage, apartment, condo being rented in this fashion is one less rental in our
inventory for local families or residents looking for any kind of.. . affordable home.

to address many of the same overtourism impacts that your bill addresses.

government has tools like your Bill 148 to do that job. In regard to the idea that a
moratorium violates the hotels due process rights, you get due process when you have
a property right issue. There's no property right in expecting you'll get a building permit.
There's no property right in expecting laws to never change, otherwise you'd never be
able to pass laws.
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
January 7, 2022
Page 113
Quoting the U.S. Supreme Court "Land-use regulations are ubiquitous and most of
them impact property values in some tangential way-often in completely unanticipated
ways. Treating them all as per se takings would transform government regulation into
a luxury few governments could afford." That was Tahoe-Sierra Preservation Council
in 2002, which is also about a moratorium on development.
And all I want to say there is a lot... is a taking only in the extraordinary circumstance
and no productive or economically beneficial use of land is permitted, which is not
what's happening here. To the point that Maui has less hotel units and more STR's,
economic Professor Dick Mayer also pointed out that Maui's resorts are not locally
owned, they are owned by foreign corporations and hedge funds. So the actual
economic impacts cannot be assumed to stay in Maui.
Also, this Council restricted hotel construction before in 1991 when you passed
Ordinance 1997 to impose interim restrictions on hotel development and the sky did
not fall on tourism. Pressing pause on issuing more permits for hotel units ending
further investigation is a necessary precaution and makes more sense than building
more hotels and just assuming the investigative group will come up with a solution to
address the impacts of expanding tourism later. That is putting the cart before the
horse and poor planning. Thank you.

We also don't have the infrastructure which is a large part of what's being talked about

MR. DANIEL LOGTENBERG. (testifying on County Communication No. 22-9):
Thank you. Chairman and Members, for allowing me to testify about CC 22-9 today.
My name is Daniel Logtenberg and I'm not a paid lobbyist. I'm heavily invested in the
tourism industry due to the number of responsibilities I have, including as a tour
operator, businessowner, a travel agency businessowner that specializes in vacation
packages, and the owner of an advertising and marketing agency based here on Maui.
I'm well aware of the challenges each sector faces, and nothing operates in a vacuum,
but we sometimes need to put a break on actions to give us time to adjust to new
realities. The pandemic has been a missed opportunity for us to review some of the
tourism impacts naturally, so we must force an unnatural pause instead through a
moratorium. Every new construction should take into account our reefs, landscape,
and community impact. I don't think the Planning Departments are rigorous enough in
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
January 7, 2022
Page 120
the compliance checking, so it's best not to allow new construction on that scale right
now.
As a marketing agency, the insight I have spans across all island industries, including
to our construction industry clients. The pandemic was probably one of the most
profitable times in their history in comparison to every other sector on the island. They
adapted quickly to new ways of operation and continued to attract new workers to the
islands. They could, could not keep the workers because of the high cost of living, so
if they're not busy building hotels for the next two years, then that will incentivize us to
get to the approvals and planning done for homes.
As a travel agency that books thousands of hotel rooms a year, I have not had any
issue booking guests into rooms. We do not need more of the same hotel rooms
perse, and the incredible prices increases we've had are not entirely market driven.
So no amount of new hotels will change that. We need hotels built that cater to people
who are looking for the VRBO or Airbnb type experience. That is what the tourism
industry is marketing, yet we are giving them hotel room boxes with one window and
no connection to the people or the island. Until hotels make the change in their
offerings, it's ridiculous to think that short-term rentals in our neighborhoods will stop.
None of the hotel's plans will solve that so why continue to serve a market with styles
of hotels that are not serving the community or the industry well?
The issue I have as a tour operator and businessowner with a staff of over 25 is that I
can't service more tourism visitors if I don't have a stable staff. We are trying to create
solid middle income class jobs, but they can't live comfortably so they don't stay.
Training and getting people up to speed is very expensive for local businesses, so
that's a huge loss to us each time. The housing options for middle class families that
work in the tourism industry is terrible. They must have roommates or be in cramped
housing that is way too expensive for how much the market says they should demand.
We will never solve this problem completely, but what, but we have to give it more than
lip service, and I think a moratorium shows that we are prepared to do something about
it. Part of the lack of housing is due the ability of nonresidents to own and operate
multiple short-term accommodations ... so I ask you, please continue to support the
moratorium and override the veto. Thank you.

MR. JEROME KEKIWI JR., (testifying on Resolution No. 22-4):
Hello. You guys can hear me? Alright, yeah. My name is Jerome Kekiwi Jr. here to
testify on Resolution 22-4. I'm the President of Na Moku Aupuni o Ko'olau Hui and our
members are lineal descendants and current residents of Keanae-Wailuanui, East
Maui. We grow taro, fish, hunt, and gather to feed our families as a way of life. We
are the lead plaintiffs in the East Maui water cases, including June 2018 decision that
restored East Maui streams.
We would like to thank our elected Councilmember Shane Sinenci for his strong
leadership on this issue. We believe he has done as much as humanly possible to do
the right things for his constituents. Thank you, Councilman. Sinenci.
In testimony before the Board of Land and Natural Resources last year on whether
EMI's final EIS should be accepted, we acknowledged the good work and relationship
that our community has recognized and developed with Mahi Pono. However, we did
express concerns that the BLNR not turn, turn over control of this critical public trust
resource to a foreign based pension investment fund whose business model includes
acquisition of water rights both here and abroad, for profit.
We agree with this resolution's basic premise, which is that of fiduciary policy and
decision makers, this Council is obligated to act foremostly in the best interest of its
constituents who are also public trust beneficiaries.
The truth is that for many decades our community suffered great injury, acknowledged
in both court and Commission on Water Resource Management decisions, under Mahi
Pono's predecessor EMI, as well as County, who were allied against us in our water
struggles. Before this, it was very difficult for our community to side with either Mahi
Pono or the County.
What is proposed in this resolution looks good, but politics begin, what they are, and
in spite of our best efforts, too often we have seen good public policy, and practice,
shift to bad after an election. So we are hoping that all of you who believe that public
trust resources should be managed by Hawaii, and not foreign corporations, will
continue to serve on this Council.
After many decades of fighting all the powers that be, the State, County, labor unions,
big corporations, their vast networks of supporters, here is our, what our community
can say for certain. Hawaii Public Trust resources should be controlled by Hawaii. It
is dangerous to surrender this trust obligation to a foreign for-profit corporation. Our
disadvantaged position relative to the global market is readily apparent with our
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
January 7, 2022
Page 122
housing crisis. Foreign investors with unlimited cash and literally pricing local people
out of their homeland.
What we want is a community partnership, management, and stewardship. It's going
on four years since streams were restored in our moku, and we have endeavored to
do all within our power and means to be good stewards. This is not a business
proposition for us, but one of sacred kuleana, and our community has been diligently
exploring various models and viable alternatives.
Our community's mission is to be good stewards of the land and its resources.
Whatever happens today, we would like to have a stronger and more cooperative
relationship with all the stakeholders, to work together to take care of the land and
water. In the past, efforts at this have been blocked. We remain open to working with
all stakeholders to move forward in a pono way. Thank you for this opportunity to
testify. Mahalo.
CHAIR LEE: Thank you. Members, any questions?
If not. Madam Clerk. Thank you, Mr. Kekiwi.

less than 150,000.
And it says we have almost 70,000 daily visitors  aday. And that's not true either. In,
in 2019, when it references we had an average census count of 66,363. So it's
important to get these things right so the people understand exactly what we are talking
about. It's also important to understand that today, this past year, our average census
of visitors was 53,289. It's almost right in line with the three to one that we are
supposed to be striving for. So taking one year separately from others is really not the
right way to go about this.
And I looked at the last 25 years and I've given you guys this in testimony before and
in 16 of the last 25 years we have exceeded that three to one resident to visitor ratio.
So it's not unusual for us to exceed that and that is going to change, you know, year to
year depending on economic conditions and so forth. But I will note that the years that
we were underneath it were recessionary years where some of what is being said was
bared out, that, that, that if we don't continue to have a certain number of visitors, we
will have a recession and we will not have enough jobs for everyone. Right now we're
in a position that, you know, is, is completely different than anything with, that we've
experienced before.

January 7, 2022
Page 133
One of the things that I keep on, on pushing with respect to this is that the number of

nothing in this Bill is going to change that. It, it includes probably 10 to 15,000 of those
daily visitors are our part time residents. And when I say part time residents, people
who have bought homes in Maui, and over this past year there have been an awful lot
of them who have bought homes in Maui as second homes and use them just for their
own use, not as illegal short-term rentals, but for their own use. And they're making up
a larger and larger percentage of our, of our visitors. So, saying you can't build another
hotel is not going to address that as, as an ongoing issue.

subcontractors. The company was established by my family, and we've been living

immediate and long-term impacts of a moratorium adding to the chaos and inflation we

MR. ALBERT PEREZ (testifying on County Communication No. 22-9 and Resolution
No. 22-4):
Aloha, Councilmembers. Good evening. Uh, aloha ahiahi, I think is what you'd say.
Anyway, I'm Albert Perez, I'm with Maui Tomorrow and I'm testifying first on Resolution
22-4. I'd just like to say that it's very risky and poor policy to continue Maui County's
reliance on a water supply that is subject to the whims of for-profit corporations. With
climate change, we're seeing more drought, alternating with rain bombs. If and when
another rain bomb causes a big landslide that blocks the ditches and the corporate
owners think it's too expensive to fix, will they just walk away. If corporate ownership
changes and the new owners have different priorities, will the County still get water?
County acquisition of water systems, like the EMI System and Wailuku Water
Company, would ensure that public interests are given priority instead of hoping that
the owners will continue to give us water. We need local control of our most precious
resource.
The system should be acquired by the County and operated by a nonprofit water
system administrator which should include the current staff who know the systems. By
administering the water systems consistent with the public trust, all users could get fair
water allocations and our public water supply won't be at risk. I ask the Council to
support this resolution and I hope that you can pass it today in order to influence
decisions that will be made very soon regarding water leases for East Maui.
Moving onto County Communication 22-9, Bill 148.  Iwould like to encourage you to
override the Mayor's veto of the moratorium. There is huge public support for an
override. But many people don't understand the need to testify over and over on the
same issue, they figure taking off work to speak once is enough, and they're not paid
to testify by development companies.
When the Mayor vetoed the previous moratorium Bill 60, the reasoning was that there
were legal problems with the Bill, and it would push development of visitor
accommodations outside West and South Maui. He said that this bill, now called Bill
148, would be better because it would not suffer from those legal problems and would
apply to the island, entire island of Maui.
Well, now he's opposing it. But the reasoning is flawed, the reasoning that he
presented in his veto message. So according to the Mayor, we need to keep building
visitor accommodations to stop illegal vacation rentals. That doesn't make sense,
especially when his Planning Department says the problem is under control. The
Mayor is ultimately responsible for enforcing the law. Is he saying that we should
expect that he won't do his job? That doesn't make sense.
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
January 7, 2022
Page 139
As far as visitor numbers right now, the Department of Business and Economic
Development and Tourism says that for November 2021, the average daily census was
57,562. With a resident population of 154,100 the number of visitors was over
37 percent in November So we are over, and this is typically a slower month for
November, slower month for tourism, over the desired 33 percent. Those who say that
this moratorium is legally flawed must have missed the excellent presentation we had,
which told us exactly how the moratorium bill must be crafted in orderto be enforceable.
And once again, this moratorium will not affect existing hotels, existing hotel
employees, or the vendors that service those hotels. These existing hotels are already
built, and they will not be affected by a pause on building new ones. In fact, they'll
benefit from reduced competition. Building visitor accommodations forever is
unsustainable and is not consistent with your support for a quality over quantity visitor
industry. We need our construction workers to help our local residents by building
affordable housing instead. And there's always lots of ongoing renovation work in
existing hotels, so please override this veto. Mahalo.

and believe an override of the Mayor's veto to establish a moratorium on new transient

Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
January 7, 2022
Page 158
The purpose of the bill to establish a moratorium to maintain the number of transient
accommodation units for a two-year period until the Council enacts legislation
addressing the findings and recommendations presented by Budget, Finance, and
Economic Development Committee established Tourism Management Temporary
Investigative Group, or two years from the enactment of the bill, whichever is sooner.
Your Committee further amended the revised bill to delete language extending the
moratorium an additional six months; delete Title 20, which was confirmed to be
redundant by the Department of the Corporation Counsel; and added an exception to
allow modifications to permits that existed prior to the effective date of the moratorium,
but do not increase the number of units. And I respectfully ask for the Members support
of my motion. Thank you.
CHAIR LEE: Alright, and then we have the seconder of the motion, Vice-Chair Rawlins-
Fernandez.
VICE-CHAIR RAWLINS-FERNANDEZ: Mahalo, Chair. Sorry, my laptop is not happy with
the, being on this meeting for so long. Okay. I strongly support the motion to override
and to file the County Communication. The olelo no'eau is he ali‘i ka ‘aina, he kauwa
ke kanaka, the aina is chief and the people her servants. Not he ali'i ka tourism
industry.
The Mayor criticized the bill for lacking transparency and public input. This bill followed
the process of going to the Maui Planning Commission, which is required for bills
affecting land use. And this Council adopted the recommendations, recommended
amendments when passing it out of PSLU Committee. So, the argument that this bill
lacks transparency in, and public input is unfounded, there has been so much public
input as demonstrated today.
In the second and final reading when we passed the bill. Member Sugimura posted an
amendment in accordance with Sunshine Law and Council Rules giving the public an
opportunity to provide feedback on the amendment being considered. What was
passed was the new affordable housing units must be physically constructed or
converted . . . the housing units currently used as transient accommodations to long
term housing for the purpose of this section.
The second amendment that was passed on second and final reading was that this
ordinance takes effect on approval and applies to new transient accommodations that
have not received its final discretionary approval prior to the effective date of this
ordinance. As Mr. Mayer said in his testimony that this was not a substantive
amendment. This is nonsubstantive and just a rearranging of words. The, what was
amended was this ordinance takes effect on approval but does not apply to the
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
January 7, 2022
Page 159
processing, approval, or granting of any application for the approval of new transient
accommodations that has received its final discretionary approval prior to the effective
date of this ordinance. So, that language just made it clear, and that’s all it was.
The moratorium would not affect existing accommodations or new accommodations
that received its final approvals before this bill. The Mayor cited the concern for further
proliferation of legal and illegal short-term rentals. It's important to note that these, that
these two should not be continued to be conflated.
The Planning Director informed us over the past three years that they have better
control over the unpermitted STR's in residential areas that require permits. The
Council has passed and considered more legislation to control STR's that do not
require permits by requiring TMKs on their advertising, for example. Mahalo Member
Paltin for introducing.
Then we heard from HHA, HHA today, that they have also been working on better
control mechanisms by clearly identifying legal and illegal STR's, and via Ms. Aiwohi,
are excited about collaborating with this County to accomplish that.
We heard a lot of testimony that this creates uncertainty for investors. To me, this
moratorium serves as a courtesy to send a strong message to the accommodations
industry that there is no uncertainty. We must better control the tourism industry that
is running over our County. For these reasons, I support the motion before us. Mahalo,
Chair.

Moratoriums are an emergency, not to be used lightly, they are an emergency process.

you're doing because what you're doing is adding to the fire. And, and we're trying to
find solutions right now.
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
January 7, 2022
Page 162
So. it, the moratorium is not something that we would go try to figure out in a TIG and
then create a moratorium. A moratorium is a stopgap so we can have this TIG and try

the entire industry that, that, you know, the goal here is to figure out how to go forward.
And maybe going forward is going to be to build, put a, a limit on how many hotels we

into creating these hourly jobs that just don't support. You can't support a family. You
can't even support yourself on one $15 an hour job. You just can't do it. You can't pay
your rent with the way the rents are.
So, I think we've been doing a good job as far as bringing more affordable housing in.
We've been addressing it that way. We've seen some of the first and lowest AMI
affordable rentals, so we've been trying to help out there. But still, I don't want people
just because now their rents lower, to still have to work two or three jobs, instead of
four or five, you know. So, this is a larger problem and I, and I think the only way to
put a hold on things while we figure out solutions is the moratorium. That's, that's why
I proposed it originally.
And this, where we're at today is we're even worse because now we have more data
since we reopened, you know, and then we caused another surge. So, we're not done
with this, with this Coronavirus. But where we still are going to be dealing with, with
health issues, because of the fact that we put our economy first. So, we haven't, we
haven't locked down the way we were originally. When we first, when we first got this

everything down.

Resolution 22-4.
Discussion, Member Sinenci.
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
January 7, 2022
Page 185
COUNCILMEMBER SINENCI: Thank you, Chair. And this item doesn't need to be, to waive
Rule 7.G. because it's just urging, it's an urging resolution and doesn't have the force
and effect of law.
Members, at the November 16, APT Committee meeting on East Maui water, a
representative of DLNR, Mr. Ian Horikawa [sic], stated that the State does not want to
see any municipal entity, such as the County of Maui compete with the private interests
of the lease. And that the County would not be required to go through the public auction
process as mandated by HRS 171, should the County so choose to, but instead, could
enter into an intergovernmental agreement with the State.
The Department of Hawaiian Homes also supports the counties acquiring the licenses.
They recognize the governmental obligation to uphold the public trust doctrine and the
legal obligations outlined in the Hawaiian Homes Commission Act.
The Department of Land and Natural Resources also stated that they are in a place
they never have been before, and that they are at ground zero in developing a lease
process. One of the reasons DLNR is having such a difficult time obtaining an
appraisal of the water rights is that the public trust doctrine rests upon the notion that
water is to be used for the public good. When a private for-profit entity acquires the
right to water, water then becomes an economic commodity in stark contrast to a public
good. With the leases managed by municipal, by a municipal public entity like
ourselves . . . congruity is solved.
An intergovernmental agreement with the State will also provide for equitable water
rates for all users, and not just for County of Maui users. These equitable water rates
will provide parity among farmers. It is unrealistic to expect full island sustainability
and a robust local farming industry when an international company is receiving heavily
subsidized water rates, and local farmers are forced to pay much more.
So, Members, it is imperative that we take this action now to protect the County's
access to water over the next 30 years. I think if we wait, we lose this opportunity for
a long time, and a lot can, can change in those 30 years.
I believe Member Rawlins-Fernandez may have some amendments for you to consider
tonight. Thank you, Chair.
CHAIR LEE: Member Vice-Chair Rawlins-Fernandez.
VICE-CHAIR RAWLINS-FERNANDEZ; Mahalo, Chair. I wholeheartedly support Member

VICE-CHAIR RAWLINS-FERNANDEZ: Mahalo, Chair. Members, these proposed bills would
amend the Fiscal Year 2022 Budget, Appendix A, Part I, Appendix D, to add an
appropriation entitled "State of Hawaii - Homeowner Assistance Fund", in the amount
of $8,060,550, along with conditional language limiting salaries and premium pay to
one limited term appointment.
These funds are to be provided to eligible entities for the purpose of preventing
homeowner mortgage delinquencies, defaults, forfeitures, loss of utility, utilities or
home energy services, and replacements [sic] of homeowners experiencing financial
hardship after January 21, 2020, through qualified expenses related to mortgage and
housing.
Again, Budget Director Yoshimura is on the line. Mahalo, Chair.
CHAIR LEE: Director, where is this money coming from?
BUDGET DIRECTOR: Chair, it's coming from the American Rescue Plan Act.
CHAIR LEE: And how much is left of the ARPA?
BUDGET DIRECTOR: So, this is a separate appropriation from our ARPA funding. This is
an appropriation of $8 million, of which $1.1 million can be used for administrative
costs. I can give you more information about the program. It's for those who have
experienced hardship because of the Coronavirus pandemic and can be used to
provide homeowners with incomes at or below 150 percent of the median, area median
income. So, 150 percent AMI for a family of four is $154,950. The total maximum
subsidy is $30,000 per household and can be used for their primary residence only.
So, this period runs through September 30 of 2026. And the types of financial
assistance that's eligible is mortgage reinstatement, mortgage payments, mortgage
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
January 7, 2022
Page 250
default resolution, property charge default resolution, and utility payment assistance.
So, once this is approved, we'll put together a program, and have it administered, and
that would be by application.

BUDGET DIRECTOR: Thank you.

Hawaii's economy is rebounding as visitors are returning to the islands following nearly two
challenging years for our visitor industry. However, they aren't only staying in our hotels and resorts
but booking short-term rentals and staying in our neighborhoods.
Recently, Maui Mayor Mike Victorino vetoed a bill  - Bill 148 - that put a moratorium on the
construction and renovation of hotels on Maui. This would only push visitors further toward short
term rentals in residential neighborhoods, resulting in fewer homes and driving up housing prices in
the middle of a housing crisis on Maui.
in 2016, out-of-state owners controlled 30 percent of Maui's total housing stock. And 14,000 of
those homes were available for rent when not occupied by the owner, 55 percent of which were
offered as short-term rentals for visitor use. This moratorium only creates an opportunity for outside
investors and homeowners to purchase and drive up housing costs for Maui residents.
1
Tell the Maui Council to protect our neighborhoods and our economy by voting NO on the
moratorium on visitor accommodations now.
Maui Home Ownership by Residency

■ Out-of-State Owner
We need policies and solutions that will continue to grow our economy, invest in sustainable
development, and make it easier for residents to stay and thrive. This moratorium is not what our
economy needs right now. We should be focused on reviving our most important industries and
investing in our neighborhoods, communities, and people.
With smart policies, we can create greater economic opportunities for people to stay here in Hawaii
AND protect the quality of life that we all cherish.

Where are they going to go now if there are no more hotel rooms? They are

RESOLUTION NO. 22-4 (2022)
Aloha Chair Lee, Vice Chair Rawlins-Fernandez, and Members of the County Council:
The Department of Hawaiian Home Lands (DHHL) submits testimony offering
comments on and support for this resolution.
The availability of water is one of the key barriers DHHL faces in the
development and delivery of homesteads across Hawai'i. In acknowledgement of that
reality, the Legislature has included a number of provisions in various statutes to help
address that challenge. All of these provisions are intended to ensure that the purposes
of the Hawaiian Homes Commission Act (HHCA), including “Providing adequate
amounts of water and supporting infrastructure, so that homestead lands will always be
usable and accessible” (HHCA 101(b)(4)) will be fulfilled.
One of those provisions relates to the issuance of state water leases and
licenses, which includes the East Maui waters addressed by this resolution. Hawai'i
Revised Statutes 171-58(g) requires that prior to the issuance of any long-term water
lease, “After consultation with affected beneficiaries, these departments shall jointly
develop a reservation of water rights sufficient to support current and future homestead
needs. Any lease of water rights or renewal shall be subject to the rights of the
department of Hawaiian home lands as provided by section 221 of the Hawaiian Homes
Commission Act.”
To fulfill this requirement, in December 2020 DHHL, after beneficiary consultation
and approval by the Hawaiian Homes Commission, has submitted a request to the
Commission on Water Resources Management (CWRM) for such a reservations We
have requested CWRM to approve a reservation of 11,177,500 gallons per day
originating from the watershed of, and tributaries to, the East Maui streams diverted by
the East Maui Irrigation system for non-potable water use in Pulehunui and Keokea-
Waiohuli Hawaiian Home Lands.
It is also helpful in relationship to this resolution to share that to further ensure
that adequate water will be provided for Hawaiian Home Lands, the Hawai'i Supreme
Court has explicitly included that reservations of water for, and uses of water by, the
Department of Hawaiian Home Lands
Page 2
DHHL for homesteading purposes are one of the four “public trust” uses of water that
are to be provided for and protected. This status is described in the same decisions
that this resolution references, the Waiahole water case and the Kaua'i Springs case (In
re Water Use Permit Applications.94 Hawaii 97 (2000), and Kauai Springs, Inc. v.
Planning Commission of County of Kauai,133 Hawaii 141(2014)).
As a municipal government agency, the County of Maui has an obligation to
uphold not only the public trust doctrine, but also the legal obligations outlined in the
Hawaiian Homes Commission Act, a Constitutional mandate and a condition of
statehood. DLNR also holds those mandates and must require of any potential water
lessee or assignee to fulfill these duties as a condition of any lease or license. We note
that the current owners of EMI participated in our beneficiary consultation process and
have done nothing to indicate that they oppose the granting of our reservation and
cooperation in the provision of water to our beneficiaries.
However, DHHL notes that the obligations of the County of Maui are
unambiguous in regard to its duties to the HHCA. Hence DHHL recognizes the benefits
that would accrue to its beneficiaries if this resolution were passed and its actions
contemplated were adopted.
Finally, we note that in separate constitutional and statutory provisions, DHHL is
to receive, in addition to any reserved water, 30% of the revenues generated by water
lease and license payments. Thus we are grateful for and support the provisions of this
resolution acknowledging that lease payments would still be made to the State.
We would be happy to answer any questions. Thank you for your consideration
of our testimony.
Me ke aloha.
William^ Aiia, Jp^Chairman
Hawaiian Homes Commission

Testimony regarding Bill 148, County Communication 22-9
Submitted by Nick Drance
The Transient Visitor Accommodations Moratorium has overwhelming public support. As you know, Covid gave
residents the opportunity to see what life here on our island looks like when it's not overrun with tourists. We need a
breather. Time to recalibrate and really discuss the future of this island. Please honor the wishes of the great majority of
your constituents.
These points have been made repeatedly but deserve to be mentioned again.
1. As has been said over and over, The Maui Island Plan stipulates a much lower tourist to resident ratio than we have
now. The Core Principles and Mission Statements also say that residents wish Maui to maintain a small town
atmosphere, which of course makes sense. Who would wish to fight traffic and be overrun with tourists? As we look
around us today, 1 wonder why we pretend that these documents have any meaning. It seems to me that a vote against
this measure says that they do not.
2. If the tourist to resident ration is already out of whack, why make a bad situation worse? That just doesn't make
sense. Of course, we can continue to build homes for more mainlanders to move here which reduces the ratio. That's
what we are doing but that doesn't make sense either.
3. Construction Industry representatives have clearly testified that there is no shortage of work for those in the
construction industry. To say that the moratorium is harmful to business is worse than inaccurate.
4. The proposal, as written, to some degree, is actually, written in favor of developers. It sanctions some development,
currently planned, or in their infancy. Projects currently submitted for approval, appear to be grandfathered in. This
looks like a clear give away to me. This measure seems tame compared to what many think it should be. Surely these
concessions should satisfy those who doubt the fairness of how this measure is written.
5. As you know, I lament the urbanization of our island, mainland style. It's not "Island style". Whether it's additional
transient or long term visitor accommodations. It seems that a mainland business model of "development" is solidly
established here. (One that primarily benefits mainland corporations and people that don't live here yet). I'll never
understand how anyone can think that "paving over paradise" makes sense on this 750 square mile island. How odd that
mainland business interests continue to take and take from this place, so blatantly... and that people who live here
actually encourage it. It just doesn't seem to make sense.

Subject: Bill 148
Happy New Year Councilmembers!
We hope you had a wonderful holiday season and are excited for the new year.
We are deeply concerned by Bill 148 because of the negative consequences it will have
and feel many do not fully understand the broad reach of the accommodations industry
that shores up many other industries and our overall economy. We stand with the
community on the need to better manage tourism, diversify our economy and build
affordable housing and rentals and are actively working on and involved in programs to
help. However, Bill 148 is highly inflexible at a time when we need great flexibility and
we do not think this moratorium is the right answer. Further, there was not enough
discussion on the ramifications of this bill and we are particularly troubled by the
substantial last minute amendments that passed without thorough community review
and vetting. We are hopeful in this new year to meet with you and connect you with
industries and businesses who can share the interconnectedness between industries,
as well as the unique issues they face to provide you with needed information on how
legislation and government regulations help or hurt.
Please consider the weight of your decision as many will remember it given the
considerable effects it will have across the community. We ask that you not override
Mayor Victorino’s veto of Bill 148 tomorrow because he has been working with the
industry, understands this measure is harmful, was not fully vetted, and has far reaching
impacts.
Mahalo for the opportunity to share our thoughts with you. We are sending you best
wishes for a joyous 2022. I look forward to seeing you during BlueJeans testimony
tomorrow.

Aloha, Pamela Tumpap here providing a response to Council Member Rawlins-Fernandez's question on
the Amendments made during Second & Final Reading (after public testimony) that the Maui Chamber
of Commerce is concerned by. Mahalo for the opportunity to allow me to answer the question in the
chat.
There were 3 amendments made during Second and Final Reading by Councilmember Rawlins-
Fernandez with one initially authored by Councilmember Sugimura, however, Councilmember
Sugimura's amendment was posted on the agenda and then brought up and revised by Council Member
Rawlins-Fernandez.
● Proposed by Councilmember Rawlins-Fernandez: Amended Section F under exemptions - “Any
development that includes new housing units built, at a ratio of at least one housing unit for every
[three] one transient accommodation unit, rounding up to the nearest whole number, of the total
number of new transient accommodation..." I mentioned this in my response to Councilmember
Rawlins-Fernandez's question during the meeting. Maui Chamber of Commerce has significant concerns
with this amendment as it changed the ratio from  1for 3 to 1 for 1 which has substantial financial
implications. The bill posted with the Second and Final Reading Agenda still had the ratio at 1 for 3.
● Proposed by Councilmember Sugimura and Amended further by Councilmember Rawlins-Fernandez:
Amended Section F, Line 4 -“Housing credits may not be issued to or used by the developer to satisfy
the requirement of this subsection for any type of unit constructed. The new affordable housing units
must be physically constructed (this is where Councilmember Sugimura's amendment ended, with the
following added by Councilmember Rawlins-Fernandez) or may be converted from housing units
currently used as transient accommodations to be used as long-term housing for the purposes of this
section." While the Maui Chamber of Commerce was okay with Councilmember Sugimura's posted
amendment to clarify that the units would be physically built, we feel Councilmember Rawlins-
Fernandez's addition included an element of change that should have been previously discussed with
the industry and vetted further so the industry and community had a chance to weigh in on it before a
Floor Amendment on Second and Final Reading.
0 Councilmember Sugimura's amendment posted on the agenda sought to amend the above section,
but only to include the line "the new affordable housing units must be physically constructed."
● Proposed by Councilmember Rawlins-Fernandez: Amended Section 2 - “This ordinance takes effect
on approval and applies to any new transient accommodation that has not received its final
discretionary approval prior to the effective date of this Ordinance."
0 Prior to Second and Final Reading, Section 2 read: This Ordinance takes effect on approval, but does
not apply to the processing, approval, or granting of any application for the approval of a new transient
accommodation that has received its final discretionary approval prior to the effective date of this
Ordinance. Amended Section 2 - “This ordinance takes effect on approval and applies to any new
transient accommodation that has not received its final discretionary approval prior to the effective date
of this Ordinance." While the Maui Chamber of Commerce understands it was provided as clarifying
language, we feel more of this work should have been done earlier as the wording was different before.

Aloha,
Yesterday, you received a letter from the Maui Hotel & Lodging Association detailing our objections to
Bill 148 and requesting your vote to sustain Mayor Victorino’s veto. HLTA has an affiliate and close
working relationship with MHLA and helped craft the letter. In circulating it statewide, we found further
support for our message. Therefore, you will find below a reproduction of the original letter with
additional signatories from tourism and tourism adjacent businesses and organizations.
We again ask for your careful consideration on Bill 148. Should you have any questions, please do not
